<<EM_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 28 Apr 2026 to 30 Apr 2026

A. EM WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

EM did not deliver a synchronized growth break; it delivered a high-dispersion, policy-space erosion tape. North Asia remained the clear positive outlier, with Taiwan GDP surging on AI/semiconductor demand and China’s private manufacturing PMI accelerating sharply, while India preserved capex-led resilience despite weaker electricity and infrastructure signals. The negative growth impulse came from Mexico, where Q1 GDP contracted more than expected, and from Turkey, where trade and reserves weakened the external-defense regime.

The weekly inflation signal hardened. Brazil’s IPCA-15 soft surprise bought tactical room for a Selic cut, but IGP-M, fiscal slippage and expectations risk made the easing path more conditional. Poland CPI surprised higher, South Africa PPI confirmed pipeline pressure, and Turkey’s reserve deterioration kept credibility risk central. The final EM regime is not “weak EM”; it is differentiated EM under a supply-shock constraint: AI/export winners versus inflation-constrained easers, reserve-defense cases, and growth/inflation dilemma economies.

B. EM MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
Taiwan GDP was the strongest growth signal, with Q1 GDP at 13.69% y/y versus 11.30% expected, confirming the AI/semiconductor demand impulse. China’s RatingDog PMI at 52.2 and NBS PMI at 50.3 kept manufacturing expansion intact, while India IIP beat consensus at 4.1% with strong capital goods. Offsetting this, Mexico GDP contracted 0.8% q/q and South Korea industrial output undershot, leaving EM growth dispersed rather than uniformly strong.

Labor: -
Supporting evidence:
The only major labor block was Turkey, where headline unemployment improved to 8.1% and participation rose to 52.8%, but labor underutilization jumped to 31.5%. The live-file read treated this as a false dawn: narrow labor optics improved, but broad slack worsened, reinforcing weak-quality domestic stabilization under an externally constrained macro regime.

Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
Inflation pressure broadened across several EMs. Brazil IPCA-15 undershot expectations but accelerated to 0.89% m/m and 4.37% y/y, while IGP-M jumped 2.73% and strengthened the pipeline-inflation signal. Poland CPI surprised higher at 3.2% y/y, South Africa PPI beat expectations on both m/m and y/y measures, and China’s private PMI carried strong input-price pressure despite stronger orders.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
Brazil cut Selic by 25bp to 14.50%, but the easing signal was conditional rather than relaxed after IPCA-15, IGP-M and fiscal slippage. Turkey’s reserves fell, with net FX reserves dropping to 54.23B from 58.38B, while the trade deficit widened to -11.220B. South Africa credit slowed to 8.50% versus 9.61% expected, and Mexico’s GDP contraction increased easing pressure while leaving Banxico credibility risk unresolved.

Policy Constraint: +
Supporting evidence:
Policy constraints tightened across the week. Brazil can still ease, but only with more credibility risk; Poland’s CPI surprise narrows NBP easing space; South Africa’s weak credit/growth mix is offset by rising PPI; Turkey’s external-defense regime is constrained by reserves and trade; Mexico faces the sharpest growth/inflation policy dilemma after the GDP contraction. China and Taiwan are the exceptions, where growth strength reduces the need for policy support rather than forcing easing.

C. FULL RELEASE ARCHIVE

India — Industrial Output YY (Mar)
Release Date: 28 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 12:30
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Industrial Output YY: 4.1% | Prior: 5.2% | Revised: 5.1% | Poll: 3.7% | Surprise: 0.40%

Professional Market Commentary

The professional tape split between the headline slowdown and the resilience surprise. Reuters framed March IIP as a five-month low, with softer factory output and weak power generation weighing on growth, but the print still beat the 3.7% Reuters poll. MoSPI’s official release confirmed 4.1% y/y IIP growth, with manufacturing at 4.3%, mining at 5.5% and electricity at only 0.8%; capital goods rose 14.6%. (reuters.com
)

The scout sharpens the institutional interpretation: public headlines emphasized “five-month low / fiscal-year-end softening,” while the better macro read is “moderation, but composition more resilient than feared.” ICRA’s Aditi Nayar, as relayed in the scout, emphasized the upside versus pessimistic house expectations, the resilience of capital goods, and the infrastructure/construction slowdown tied to softer steel and cement output. The key tension is therefore not collapse versus strength, but resilient capex versus electricity and infrastructure bottlenecks.

Comment — Economist Layer

The hierarchy is: upside surprise first, but momentum moderation second and more important for the forward path. A 4.1% print above consensus and above the weaker institutional pre-release fears says the Middle East / West Asia transmission was not acute enough in March to break headline activity. But the internals are no longer clean: electricity slowed sharply, infrastructure/construction goods moved down to 6.7% after 11.1% in February per the scout, and Q4 FY26 IIP growth moderated to 4.8% from 5.3% in Q3.

The durable positive signal is capital goods. Two consecutive double-digit capital-goods prints keep the investment-cycle story alive and prevent this release from reading as a broad India growth break. The negative signal is the power / infrastructure complex: electricity at 0.8% and softer steel/cement-linked construction goods are the first places where the supply shock and operational-bottleneck channel can show up before it becomes visible in headline demand.

State transition: continuation

Context from prior regional live file: India had entered the week as one of the cleaner EM quality-growth stories, with the April flash PMI beat reinforcing strong activity but also showing precautionary inventory-building and rising input-cost pressure. March IIP preserves that broad resilience story, but weakens the clean-growth version through electricity and infrastructure softness. The India regime remains resilient growth under a rising supply-side constraint, not a growth break.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

The RBI implication is neutral-to-hawkish relative to the slowdown headline. The print does not give enough activity weakness to reopen an easy easing narrative, because manufacturing and mining held up better than feared and capital goods still point to investment resilience. But it adds a slow-burn deterioration vector: if April–June data show electricity, infrastructure and margin pressure worsening under the Middle East / Hormuz shock, the India macro story moves from quality-growth outperformer to supply-constrained resilience.

For INR, the print is still supportive at the structural level because capex has not broken, but the risk channel is now clearer. India is not trading as a pure high-growth / benign-inflation EM story anymore; it is trading as a high-quality growth story with a visible imported-cost and real-economy bottleneck overlay.

Brazil — IPCA-15 Mid-Month CPI / IPCA-15 Mid-Month CPI YY (Apr)
Release Date: 28 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 14:00
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

IPCA-15 Mid-Month CPI: 0.89% | Prior: 0.44% | Poll: 1.00% | Surprise: -0.110%
IPCA-15 Mid-Month CPI YY: 4.37% | Prior: 3.90% | Poll: 4.49% | Surprise: -0.120%

Professional Market Commentary

The institutional tape framed the release as tactical relief, not benign inflation. IBGE reported IPCA-15 at 0.89% m/m, up from 0.44%, with the 12-month rate rising to 4.37% from 3.90%; food and beverages were the largest positive contribution. Reuters emphasized that the print undershot the 1.0% poll and reinforced expectations for a 25bp Copom cut to 14.50%. (agenciadenoticias.ibge.gov.br
)

The scout adds the key divergence: the monthly print was softer than feared, but still the steepest IPCA-15 reading since February 2025, while the 12-month rate moved up toward the top of the tolerance band. BofA, as captured in the public tape, revised near-term April IPCA lower after softer airfares and rents, but raised inflation in the monetary-policy horizon to 3.5% from 3.3%, reflecting food, electricity-tariff and fuel-pass-through risks. (investinglive.com
)

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the week’s cleanest false-dawn print. The mechanical surprise is dovish: 0.89% versus 1.00% and 4.37% versus 4.49% allowed the market to treat the release as sufficient cover for another 25bp cut. The macro composition is not dovish: the monthly pace accelerated sharply, the 12-month rate rose materially, food-at-home accelerated, and the price shock remains concentrated in food/fuel/relative-price channels. That is not demand overheating, but it is exactly the supply-shock mix that can keep expectations drifting even if one print undershoots consensus.

The hierarchy is therefore: Copom tactical room first, expectations risk second, supply-shock persistence third. The print buys time for the easing cycle because high real rates and softer-than-feared underlying items still matter. But it does not validate a clean disinflation path because expectations have moved higher and the relevant policy horizon is getting worse, not better. The key macro point is that the inflation process is becoming less about the April headline miss and more about whether food, fuel and tariff shocks become embedded in expectations.

State transition: false dawn

Context from prior regional live file: Brazil had previously sat in the “more room, less external stress” EM bucket, with external financing still manageable and policy flexibility better than in more fragile reserve-constrained cases. April IPCA-15 narrows that comfort because inflation expectations and supply-side pass-through are now doing more of the constraining work. Brazil remains better placed than Turkey-style external stress, but the easing window has become more conditional and less benign.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is a Copom-guidance event more than a CPI event. A 25bp cut can still be defended because the print undershot expectations and real rates remain high, but the forward language should matter more than the move. A cautious cut is consistent with the data; a dovish cut would look poorly anchored against the rising 12-month rate, food/fuel pressure and expectations drift.

For BRL and Brazil rates, the front-end can trade the soft surprise, but the long end should be less willing to rally unless the BCB contains the expectations channel. The key asymmetry is that the print reduces near-term pause panic but increases the importance of the statement.

The public tape framed the release as a split labor print. TurkStat reported a lower seasonally adjusted unemployment rate and higher labor-force participation, while broader underutilization rose sharply. Employment rose and the narrow unemployment rate improved, but the broad slack measure prevents the release from being a clean labor-market-strength signal.

Comment — Economist Layer

The analytical hierarchy is broad slack over headline unemployment. Lower unemployment with higher participation is superficially constructive, and the increase in employment prevents the print from being dismissed as pure labor-force arithmetic. But the jump in underutilization to 31.5% changes the macro read: Turkey is still carrying a large hidden-labor-slack problem even as the narrow unemployment rate improves.

State transition: false dawn

Context from prior regional live file: Turkey’s prior EM sequence had already shifted from a simple tight-policy stabilization story toward external fragility, reserve sensitivity and inflation/import-pressure constraints. March labor data do not overturn that regime: the headline unemployment improvement helps optics, but the broad underutilization jump keeps the domestic adjustment incomplete. The Turkish macro mix remains externally constrained with weak-quality labor stabilization.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is not a CBRT easing signal. In a normal cycle, falling unemployment with rising participation would lower the urgency for policy support; in Turkey’s current regime, the more important point is that broad labor slack remains high while the binding constraint is still inflation credibility and reserves. For TRY, the release is secondary to the 30 Apr reserve and trade data, but it matters as a political-economy warning.

This is a pipeline-inflation confirmation block. IGP-M is not the policy target, but a 2.73% monthly rise immediately after the IPCA-15 acceleration matters because it confirms that the supply shock is not isolated to one consumer basket. State transition: escalation

Context from prior regional live file: Brazil’s earlier EM history already showed upstream inflation re-entering the system through the March IGP-DI reversal and consumer inflation confirmation. HF Take — Strategist Layer

This matters for the long end and for Copom language. The Selic move can still be 25bp, but the BCB cannot credibly describe the inflation backdrop as cleanly improving. For BRL, the key risk is not this single IGP-M print in isolation; it is the interaction of upstream price pressure, food/fuel pass-through and expectations drift.

Brazil — Selic Interest Rate (29 Apr)
Release Date: 29 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 23:30
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

Selic Interest Rate: 14.50% | Prior: 14.75% | Poll: 14.50% | Surprise: 0.000%

Professional Market Commentary

The BCB cut the Selic by 25bp to 14.50%, in line with consensus and for the second consecutive meeting. Reuters framed the decision as a cautious cut under Middle East / Iran-war uncertainty, with Copom leaving next moves open and stressing that the future path depends on geopolitical and economic developments. The committee remained restrictive despite the cut, with inflation still above target and forecasts revised higher. (reuters.com
)

Comment — Economist Layer

The move validates the scout’s Brazil read: the soft IPCA-15 surprise bought tactical room, but not strategic comfort. A unanimous 25bp cut says Copom is still willing to ease into restrictive real-rate territory, but the lack of stronger forward guidance says the cycle is no longer automatic. The decision is therefore not dovish in isolation; it is a cautious continuation of easing under worsening uncertainty.

State transition: continuation with higher conditionality

Context from prior regional live file: Brazil had previously been one of the EM cases with more policy room because external stress was less acute than in reserve-constrained peers. The IPCA-15 / IGP-M / Selic sequence keeps that relative advantage, but it narrows the margin: Brazil can still cut, but only with a visibly restrictive and conditional reaction function. The regime shifts from “easing-compatible” to “easing, but increasingly hostage to supply shocks and expectations.”

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is the central Brazil event of the week. Front-end rates can accept the cut because it was expected and because IPCA-15 undershot. But the curve and BRL should focus on the conditionality: if inflation expectations and IGP-style pipeline pressure keep rising, the BCB loses room to continue easing without damaging credibility. June becomes the next reaction-function test, not a mechanical continuation point.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

If Beijing sees export activity holding up, the bar for broad monetary easing rises. Professional Market Commentary

Reuters framed Mexico Q1 GDP as a larger-than-expected contraction, with the economy shrinking 0.8% q/q versus a 0.5% expected decline and growing only 0.1% y/y versus 0.8% expected. INEGI data showed weakness across sectors, with primary, secondary and tertiary activity all contracting on the quarter. (reuters.com
)

Comment — Economist Layer

This is a clean growth deterioration print. The weakness is broad enough to matter for Banxico because it arrives after prior inflation and PPI prints had already complicated the easing path. Mexico is therefore not a simple dovish-growth story: the growth side is weak, but the price side has not given Banxico a clean green light. The GDP miss intensifies the central-bank dilemma rather than resolving it.

State transition: deterioration

Context from prior regional live file: Mexico’s prior EM file already placed Banxico in one of the sharpest credibility binds: inflation and PPI had risen while the board was divided over cuts. Q1 GDP adds a hard-growth deterioration to that existing inflation-policy tension. Mexico now looks like the clearest EM stagflation-policy dilemma in this week’s tape.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is MXN and rates relevant. Growth weakness supports the easing argument, but it does not remove the inflation constraint. The tactical read is front-end easing pressure; the strategic read is higher policy-error risk if Banxico cuts too easily into an inflation-expectations problem. Mexico now belongs high in the 3-key-release follow-up list.

For ZAR, this is a mild offset to the PPI and credit negatives. It does not change the SARB reaction function, but it helps prevent a balance-of-payments stress narrative. The market read is relative resilience rather than outright bullishness.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: EM headline growth is still supported by North Asia tech/export strength, but policy space is being eroded by inflation, reserves and fiscal constraints elsewhere.
Why it matters: China/Taiwan are growth-positive, while Brazil, Poland, Mexico and Turkey are becoming harder reaction-function cases for different reasons.
Implication: Trade EM through sub-regimes: AI/export winners, cautious-inflation easers, external-defense cases, and growth/inflation dilemma cases.

3 KEY RELEASES TO DIG INTO

Brazil — Selic Interest Rate (29 Apr)
Why it matters: The cut confirms that Copom can still ease, but only in a much more conditional regime after IPCA-15 false-dawn relief, IGP-M pipeline pressure and fiscal slippage. The policy signal is not the 25bp move; it is how long the BCB can keep easing without losing inflation-expectations credibility.
China — RatingDog Manufacturing PMI Final (30 Apr)
Why it matters: This is the strongest China/Asia signal in the week: private manufacturing accelerated sharply and beat consensus, confirming export and order momentum. The complication is that input-price pressure and employment lag make it a growth-positive but not cleanly benign print.
Mexico — GDP YY Flash / GDP QQ Flash (30 Apr)
Why it matters: The Q1 contraction creates the clearest Banxico dilemma in the EM file. Growth is weak enough to support easing, but prior inflation/PPI pressure and board division mean the easing path now carries higher credibility risk.

<<EM_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 20 Apr 2026 to 24 Apr 2026

A. EM WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

EM resolved into a higher-dispersion, higher-quality-growth week at the aggregate level, but not an easier policy regime. The strongest signals came from North Asia and India: Taiwan export orders and industrial output, Korea Q1 GDP, and India flash PMIs all confirmed that AI capex, semiconductors, facility investment, and precautionary manufacturing demand are still lifting the real side. Poland added a second upside bucket, with industrial output, retail sales, and wages shifting the local regime toward broader activity strength and hawkish composition drift.

The offset was that policy space did not reopen with the growth upside. Turkey stayed in defensive stabilization through corridor mechanics and conditional reserve repair; South Africa’s benign headline CPI masked less comfortable core/services composition while retail weakened; Mexico remained the clearest structural-drag case with weak activity and still-imperfect disinflation; Brazil’s external cushion narrowed marginally through freight/services pressure. The weekly EM regime is therefore not synchronized reflation, but sub-regime dispersion: AI-tech upside, Poland real-side reacceleration, and externally/policy-constrained laggards.

B. EM MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: +
Supporting evidence:
The week delivered several high-conviction upside growth signals: Taiwan export orders surged 65.9% y/y versus 41.0% expected, Taiwan industrial output rose 28.68% y/y, Korea Q1 GDP beat materially at 1.7% q/q and 3.6% y/y, India flash composite PMI rose to 58.3, and Poland’s industrial output and retail sales both surprised strongly. The negative counterweight was Mexico IGAE at -0.3% y/y versus +0.6% expected and South Africa retail sales slowing sharply, but the weekly aggregate growth impulse was clearly stronger.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
Labor signals were mixed rather than directionally decisive. Poland showed employment still contracting at -0.9% y/y and unemployment at 6.1%, but wages reaccelerated to 6.6% y/y, making the labor backdrop less benign for inflation. Taiwan’s jobless rate rose only marginally to 3.35%, a low-information move that confirmed tight labor conditions without showing broad domestic overheating. The week did not resolve a clean EM labor-cycle signal.

Inflation: ~
Supporting evidence:
Inflation pressure was not uniformly worse, but the composition kept policy relief limited. South Africa CPI matched consensus at 3.1% y/y, yet core and services reaccelerated before the April fuel shock enters the data. Mexico’s first-half April core inflation was slightly softer than expected, preserving the easing path, but headline inflation remained distorted by food and fuel pressures. Poland PPI stayed negative but narrowed, while wages reaccelerated, leaving disinflation incomplete rather than clean.

Financial Conditions: ~
Supporting evidence:
Financial conditions stabilized tactically but remained conditional. China’s unchanged LPR kept the economy in supportive-hold mode, with likely support through targeted liquidity channels rather than broad LPR easing. Turkey’s gross FX reserves fell while net reserves rose, suggesting conditional stabilization rather than durable repair. Brazil’s current-account deficit widened and FDI missed, but the external picture remained financeable. The week therefore showed no disorderly tightening, but no broad easing impulse either.

Policy Constraint: +
Supporting evidence:
Policy constraint increased despite stronger growth optics. Turkey’s CBRT hold was effectively a defensive corridor-based stance, not a neutral hold. South Africa’s CPI composition and coming fuel shock kept SARB in hold mode despite weaker retail activity. Poland’s real-side upside and wage reacceleration made dovish NBP repricing harder. Mexico’s weak activity supports Banxico easing, but above-target and supply-distorted inflation prevents a clean growth-only reaction function. EM policy remains constrained by dispersion, inflation composition, and external fragility.

Comment — Economist Layer

The key hierarchy is resilience first, instrument choice second. The unchanged decision is consistent with a macro mix in which growth has firmed enough to remove urgency for broad-based easing, but transmission still looks too weak to justify a durable tightening story. NIM compression in the banking sector matters here: the constraint is not only whether the PBOC wants to cut, but whether LPR remains the right transmission channel. State transition: continuation.

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior EM sequence had already shifted China away from pure weak-growth support and toward a more mixed regime where firmer activity data reduced immediate easing pressure but domestic-demand quality remained incomplete. April’s unchanged LPR extends that same structure. The easing bias remains targeted rather than broad and urgent.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This keeps China in a supportive-hold regime. For CNH and China-sensitive cyclicals, the implication is that additional support is more likely to arrive through targeted liquidity tools, RRR, or 7-day reverse repo management than through another immediate benchmark-style LPR move.

This keeps SARB hold as the base case. Markets should care less about the exact 3.1% headline and more about the May CPI print, which becomes the real test of whether the supply shock stays buffered or starts to threaten the medium-term disinflation story.

Foreign Direct Investm't: 6.037B | Prior: 6.754B | Poll: 7.000B | Surprise: -0.9630B

Professional Market Commentary

The immediate narrative was factual rather than heavily interpreted: the current-account deficit came in wider than expected and FDI undershot, but the external picture was not read as acute stress. The more interesting institutional angle was the explicit reference to freight and logistics costs as a Hormuz-related services-account channel.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is a mild deterioration print, not a regime break. The current-account deficit widened, FDI missed, the trade surplus narrowed, and services costs worsened, but the 12-month coverage still says Brazil remains externally financeable rather than fragile. The important refinement is transmission: Brazil is not mainly taking the shock through direct reserve stress or immediate energy panic, but through freight and services-account pressure. State transition: continuation with mild deterioration.

Context from prior regional live file:
The previous Brazil external block had placed Brazil in the “more room, less external stress” camp rather than in the fragile-liquidity camp, with FDI still comfortably covering the deficit. March weakens that comfort at the margin but does not overturn it. Brazil remains structurally better placed than the more externally constrained EM cases, though the cushion is clearly narrowing.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is mildly BRL-negative but not enough to create a first-order external-vulnerability story. Strategically, it matters because it shows how Hormuz can still tighten Brazil’s macro mix through logistics costs even without turning Brazil into a Turkey-style external stress case.

Turkey — Gross FX Reserves / Net FX Reserves (17 Apr, w/e)
Release Date: 24 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 13:30
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Gross FX Reserves: 61.82B | Prior: 64.07B
Net FX Reserves: 58.38B | Prior: 55.59B

Professional Market Commentary

The prevailing narrative was that reserves were stabilizing under the ceasefire and that some tactical relief had returned to the system. The more sophisticated institutional read, however, kept the focus on swap-adjusted reserve quality rather than on headline weekly moves.

Comment — Economist Layer

The gross-down/net-up split is the whole story. Headline reserve optics improved on one measure and worsened on another, which strongly suggests that technical balance-sheet mechanics and swap structures still matter more than a simple “reserves recovered” headline. The right inference is conditional stabilization: the post-shock drawdown is no longer worsening in a disorderly way, but the system remains highly exposed to any renewed external stress. State transition: stabilisation — conditional.

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior EM sequence had already moved Turkey from credibility-rebuild territory toward external fragility, with reserve and current-account pressures becoming central to the macro story. This week’s reserves fit that exact pattern. The direction is better than during the peak stress phase, but the underlying buffer is still too conditional to call structurally repaired.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

TRY and CDS should still be traded off regime integrity more than off the weekly reserve headline. If ceasefire durability holds, reserves can keep stabilizing; if it breaks, the recovery narrative can reverse very quickly and pull the CBRT back toward explicit tightening pressure.

This is a clean downside activity surprise. SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: EM’s strongest weekly signals came from AI-tech and Poland’s real-side upside, but policy space did not reopen proportionately because Turkey remains in defensive stabilization, South Africa’s inflation composition turned less benign, and Mexico’s stagnation keeps the growth-inflation trade-off alive.
Why it matters: A single EM beta narrative now misprices the dispersion.
Implication: The actionable map is sub-regime based — AI-tech EM, EU-funds plus hawkish-drift EM, and structural-drag EM.

<<EM_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 13 Apr 2026 to 16 Apr 2026

A. EM WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

EM ended the week in a tighter and more stagflationary configuration, not a synchronized growth break. China still delivered enough headline activity to avoid a hard landing read, Brazil remained positive but moderating, and Korea’s export complex stayed exceptionally strong; but labor softened at the margin, domestic-demand quality remained weak in the key China package, and the inflation/external side deteriorated materially across several high-information cases.

The binding weekly message was that policy room narrowed faster than growth collapsed. India shifted from benign headline CPI comfort toward clear upstream inflation pressure via WPI; Poland moved into a more uncomfortable inflation-plus-external mix; Turkey’s reserve rebound improved tactics but did not repair the deeper current-account problem. That leaves EM less as a broad beta growth story and more as a country-by-country policy-constraint map, with inflation, external financing and energy sensitivity becoming more decisive than pure activity momentum.

B. EM MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
China’s Q1 GDP beat at 5.0% and industrial output beat, Brazil’s IBC-Br stayed positive at +0.6% m/m and services remained above pre-pandemic levels, and Korea’s export cycle stayed extremely strong. But China retail sales missed badly at +1.7%, fixed investment also missed, Brazil retail y/y slowed to +0.2%, and South Africa’s mining strength remained too narrow to imply broad EM reacceleration.

Labor: -
Supporting evidence:
The labor signal deteriorated modestly rather than collapsing. China’s urban unemployment rate ticked up to 5.4%, India’s unemployment rate rose to 5.1% with weaker labor-force participation, and the broader week increasingly pointed to softer domestic-demand transmission rather than labor-driven strength. There was no offsetting labor print strong enough to neutralize that drift.

Inflation: +
Supporting evidence:
India’s CPI remained manageable in level terms at 3.40%, but the decisive signal came from WPI at 3.88%, a large upside surprise confirming oil and upstream pipeline pressure. Poland’s CPI re-accelerated to 3.0% y/y and core rose to 2.7%, showing inflation was no longer easing cleanly. Across the file, price pressure increasingly shifted from manageable headline optics toward more binding wholesale, imported and energy-linked inflation risk.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
Turkey’s current-account deficit stayed structurally uncomfortable and Poland’s current account swung sharply into deficit, both weakening external cushions. Turkey’s later reserve rebound improved short-term optics, but the file consistently treated current-account deterioration as the deeper regime signal. The week therefore pointed to tighter effective financial conditions through external vulnerability and weaker financing comfort, not through a single uniform rate shock.

Policy Constraint: +
Supporting evidence:
India’s WPI shock effectively closed near-term RBI easing room, Poland’s inflation-plus-external mix narrowed NBP flexibility, and Turkey remained boxed in by TRY management and reserve/external constraints. China’s Q1 GDP beat reduced immediate easing pressure but did not solve the domestic-demand problem, leaving targeted support rather than broad relief as the more plausible path. Across EM, the week removed easing room more clearly than it removed growth resilience.

Turkey — Current Account Balance (Feb)
Release Date: 13 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 09:00
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

Current Account Balance: -7.501B | Prior: -6.807B | Poll: -7.500B | Surprise: -0.0010B

Professional Market Commentary

The better-anchored read was that the headline was close to consensus but the trend remained clearly worse: ING highlighted a wider 12-month rolling deficit at $35.4bn and weaker capital-flow momentum after January’s stronger inflows. The market story was therefore not the tiny miss, but the persistence of external deterioration under a high-rate regime.

Comment — Economist Layer

The key hierarchy is financing quality first, headline surprise last. A near-on-consensus monthly deficit would normally be a non-event, but the composition supplied in the scout points to a wider goods gap, weaker offset from services, and reserve pressure doing too much of the adjustment work. That means the February print does not describe stabilization; it describes an external deficit that remains structurally uncomfortable even before the full March oil shock enters the data.

State transition: deterioration

Context from prior regional live file:
Turkey had already shifted in the prior EM sequence from “tight-policy stabilization” toward “external fragility under oil/import pressure.” February current account data extends that trajectory rather than offsetting it.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This keeps CBRT boxed in. A current-account gap of this size does not by itself trigger a new policy move, but it reinforces that TRY management and reserve optics remain more binding than growth softness in the reaction function.

India — WPI Inflation YY (Mar)
Release Date: 15 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 08:30
Importance: ****

Reuters Data

WPI Inflation YY: 3.88% | Prior: 2.13% | Poll: 3.04% | Surprise: +0.84pp

Professional Market Commentary

Both the official release and Reuters framed this as the strongest wholesale inflation print in more than three years. The core message was unambiguous: oil, food and manufactured-goods prices all pushed higher, with the biggest impulse coming from energy and upstream pipeline pressure.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the first clean confirmation that the oil shock has crossed from a risk scenario into the Indian price pipeline. The size of the beat matters, but the composition matters more: wholesale inflation jumped before consumer inflation fully absorbed the shock. That means this is not primarily a demand-overheating signal. It is an energy-led cost-push shock entering the system with broader manufacturing pass-through behind it.

State transition: escalation

Context from prior regional live file:
India had been one of the relatively better EM stories, but already with oil as the obvious vulnerability. March WPI is the point at which that vulnerability ceases to be hypothetical. It materially tightens the policy constraint even though final demand had not yet broken.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This effectively closes the near-term RBI easing window. From here, the key India question is no longer whether inflation risk is rising, but how quickly WPI bleeds into CPI and how much labor/demand softness emerges at the same time.

Public real-time summaries showed IBC-Br up 0.6% m/m in February after 0.8% in January, slightly above forecast, with industry cited as an important positive contributor. The broad tape read was that activity remained positive, but was no longer accelerating.

Comment — Economist Layer

That is the right read. The beat versus consensus is not the main message; the deceleration from January is. Together with softer retail y/y and still-resilient services, February IBC-Br fits a Brazil macro profile of positive but moderating activity under high rates, not recession and not reacceleration.

State transition: continuation

Context from prior regional live file:
Brazil had already been moving through a moderation process in the prior EM sequence, but without evidence of collapse. February IBC-Br reinforces that exact path: activity is still holding, but with less thrust than at the start of the year.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This gives COPOM some comfort that activity has not broken, which limits the case for an aggressively dovish reinterpretation. Brazil remains a “rates are biting, but inflation/fiscal constraints still dominate” story.

Poland — Net Inflation YY (Mar)
Release Date: 16 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 14:00
Importance: **

Reuters Data

Net Inflation YY: 2.70% | Prior: 2.50% | Poll: 2.50% | Surprise: +0.20pp

Professional Market Commentary

NBP reported March core inflation excluding food and energy at 2.7% y/y. That leaves core below headline CPI at 3.0%, but also confirms that the underlying trend is no longer easing further.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is a mild but important deterioration. Headline inflation is still more energy-led than demand-led, yet the core pick-up from 2.5% to 2.7% says the March inflation rebound is not pure fuel noise. The gap between headline and core still gives NBP more flexibility than if core had surged, but the direction of travel is clearly less comfortable.

State transition: deterioration (mild)

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior EM sequence had already moved Poland away from an uncomplicated easing story once March CPI re-accelerated. Core at 2.7% confirms that the inflation pressure is beginning to leak beyond the most energy-sensitive components.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

NBP still has more room than the headline alone implies, but less than it had one month ago. The combination of weaker externals, firmer headline CPI and now firmer core makes Poland a tighter policy-constraint story than it looked at the end of March.

SIGNAL TENSION CHECK

Main contradiction: Headline activity is still holding in parts of EM — China GDP beat, Brazil IBC-Br stayed positive, Korea exports remain exceptional — but the underlying demand side is softening while wholesale and consumer inflation pressures are accelerating.

Why it matters: That is the textbook mix that removes easing room without delivering a clean growth recession.

Implication: EM should be read through country-level policy constraints and inflation/external vulnerability, not through a single broad-beta growth view.

3 KEY RELEASES TO DIG INTO

India — WPI Inflation YY (15 Apr 2026)
Why it matters: This is the week’s cleanest macro regime signal. The 3.88% print and large upside surprise confirm that the oil shock has entered India’s wholesale pipeline decisively, turning March CPI’s still-benign headline into a lagging rather than reassuring signal.
China (Mainland) — GDP / Activity Package (16 Apr 2026)
Why it matters: The 5.0% GDP beat matters, but the internal split matters more. Industrial output held, while retail and investment disappointed, which makes this the key China tension for the week: supply-side resilience and fiscal support against weak domestic demand.
Turkey — Current Account Balance (13 Apr 2026)
Why it matters: This is still the structural Turkey signal of the week, even after the later reserve rebound. The weekly reserve data improved the optics, but the current-account deterioration is the deeper regime marker because it speaks directly to the durability of external financing and TRY stability.

<<EM_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 6 Apr 2026 to 10 Apr 2026

A. EM WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

EM resolved into a broad supply-shock-constrained, increasingly stagflationary week rather than a clean growth slowdown or clean inflation re-acceleration. Brazil delivered the clearest deterioration in both activity and prices; Mexico delivered the sharpest policy dilemma as inflation surged into an already divided Banxico while activity weakened; India’s combination of softer services quality and an RBI hold-with-caution captured the template EM central-bank response to the shock; China shifted into “bad inflation” through positive PPI without demand-led CPI strength; Korea stayed on hold but under tighter oil/FX constraint; and South Africa’s apparent PMI resilience was undermined by weak internals and poor hard manufacturing data. The main exceptions were Turkey, where reserves and pre-shock activity improved enough to signal stabilization rather than acute stress, and Taiwan, where AI-led exports and still-soft CPI made it the lone clean positive outlier. Labor did not emerge as the dominant regional driver: evidence was sparse and mixed, with isolated survey support unable to offset the broader growth-cost squeeze.

B. EM MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
The regional growth signal weakened but did not collapse uniformly. Brazil’s services/composite PMIs deteriorated sharply, Mexico industrial output contracted more than expected, and South Africa’s PMI internals plus manufacturing output pointed to weaker underlying momentum. India services stayed in expansion but with softer domestic demand quality, while Taiwan’s export surge and Turkey’s pre-shock industrial recovery prevented a one-direction regional contraction call.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
The week did not produce a decisive regional labor signal. South Africa’s PMI reported improved employment, but that support sat alongside falling new orders, weaker exports and collapsing confidence, so it did not validate a durable improvement. Elsewhere, labor evidence was largely absent from the file, leaving labor as unresolved rather than a clean source of deterioration or support.

Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
The dominant weekly signal was tighter price pressure through multiple channels. Brazil moved from softer activity into upstream and then consumer-price re-acceleration via IGP-DI and IPCA; Mexico printed higher headline inflation and a sharp PPI rise; China’s PPI turned positive even as CPI stayed soft, signalling supply-side cost pressure; and Korea’s central bank explicitly linked the new inflation risk to oil and FX. Taiwan’s CPI undershoot was the main exception rather than the regional norm.

Financial Conditions: ~
Supporting evidence:
Financial conditions tightened through FX/oil sensitivity but not in a uniform stress pattern. India’s rupee weakness and RBI focus on containing excess FX volatility, Korea’s weakened won and market-volatility concern, and the broader oil shock all argued for tighter conditions. Offsetting that, Turkey’s large net reserve improvement and South Africa’s still-high reserve levels prevented a generalized EM balance-sheet stress conclusion.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The week clearly narrowed easing room across the region. RBI held while marking growth down and inflation up; NBP paused after March’s cut rather than validating an automatic easing path; BOK’s hold became materially less dovish; Banxico’s inflation print and 3–2 split exposed a sharper credibility bind; and Brazil’s worsening growth-price mix made any cut path more conditional. Only Taiwan remained comfortably outside the tightening-of-constraint cluster.

India — HSBC: Services - Business Activity (Mar)
Release Date: 6 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 07:00 IST
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

HSBC: SERVICES - BUSINESS ACTIVITY: 57.5 | Prior: 57.2 | Poll: 57.4 | Surprise: 0.10

Professional Market Commentary

Headline beat versus flash kept the top-line tone constructive, but the better-anchored tape was materially softer underneath: March was the weakest services expansion in 14 months, domestic new business slowed to a 14-month low, export demand stayed very strong, and input cost inflation rose to the fastest pace in 45 months. Firms also absorbed part of the cost shock rather than fully passing it through.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is still an expansion print, but the quality deteriorated for a second straight month. The hierarchy inside the release is: headline still strong, domestic demand softer, exports offsetting, and input-cost pressure intensifying. That combination matters more than the small beat versus consensus. It says India services is not breaking, but it is leaning more on external demand while domestic momentum cools and margins get squeezed.

State transition: continuation with deterioration sub-signal

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For the RBI path, this is mildly supportive of future easing only on the growth side, but the cost-pressure channel keeps that optionality conditional on oil and FX stabilising. In EM cross-section terms, India starts the week as the “still-growing but less clean” economy rather than a demand-collapse case.

Brazil — Trade Balance (Mar)
Release Date: 7 Apr 2026 | Local Time: 20:00 BRT
Importance: ***

Reuters Data

Trade Balance: 6.405B | Prior: 4.208B | Revised: 4.038B | Poll: 7.350B | Surprise: -0.9450B

The official/public tape split between two valid framings: the lowest March surplus since 2020 and, simultaneously, still a large surplus supported by very strong oil exports. The better reading is compositional. The main signal is not the surplus number itself but what drove it. Oil re-routing lifted the export side, especially to China, while the import bill surged. That means sequential improvement versus February flatters the underlying external picture. This is not a broad-based structural export-strength signal. It is a commodity-composition windfall layered on top of a much heavier import bill.

It matters more as a vulnerability flag if oil support fades while imports remain elevated.

Public macro commentary around the release emphasised that rising upstream costs were meeting still-soft downstream demand rather than demand-led inflation strength.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is “bad inflation,” not healthy reflation. PPI turning positive is structurally important because it ends a long factory-gate deflation streak. But the CPI undershoot and sharp negative monthly reading show household demand is still too soft to absorb or transmit the shock fully. That is the worst policy mix from a cyclical perspective: input costs rise, margins compress, and consumer demand remains mediocre.

State transition: reversal for PPI; continuation for CPI

Context from prior regional live file:
Raw EM GitHub live file returned empty at drafting time, so this block opens the China price-sequence inside the current EM week rather than updating a populated prior file.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This narrows room for aggressive PBOC easing and matters globally because a positive China PPI is a supply-chain and commodity-input signal, not just a domestic China fact. For the EM week, China joins the inflation-pressure side of the ledger, but via a supply-side channel rather than consumer-demand strength.

<<EM_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 30 Mar 2026 to 3 Apr 2026

A. EM WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The EM week did not produce a synchronized growth break, but it did materially worsen the quality of the macro mix. Korea, Taiwan, Brazil, Mexico and Poland manufacturing all stabilized or improved sequentially, China’s official manufacturing survey returned to expansion, and India still printed resilient hard activity and expansionary manufacturing. But that resilience was increasingly compromised by a visible imported-cost and logistics shock: China’s private PMI softened sharply against the stronger official survey, Brazil’s price pipeline re-firmed, Poland’s CPI re-accelerated, Korea’s inflation looked softer only because fuel caps muted pass-through, and Turkey’s growth side deteriorated hard just as reserves weakened materially.

The result is an EM regime of mixed activity but tighter policy constraint. Labor evidence was limited and only modestly softer where visible, yet inflation and external-balance pressure became more binding across the region than domestic-demand weakness alone. This was not a clean disinflationary slowdown week; it was a week in which easing room narrowed because the dominant marginal signal shifted toward cost-push inflation, external fragility, and more fragile policy optionality.

B. EM MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
The week’s activity signal stayed mixed rather than collapsing. Korea’s industrial output rebounded strongly m/m and PMI rose to 52.6, China’s NBS manufacturing PMI moved back above 50, Poland, Brazil and Mexico PMIs all improved sequentially, and India’s February industrial output beat consensus. But quality deteriorated in important pockets: China’s private manufacturing PMI softened sharply, India’s March PMI missed and slowed materially, Brazil’s industry remained weak in capital goods, and Turkey’s manufacturing PMI fell to 47.9, confirming a harsher slowdown.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
Labor evidence was sparse and did not define the regional week. Turkey’s unemployment rate rose to 8.5%, but participation also increased and underutilization stayed very high at 29.9%, pointing to softening without a labor-market cliff. Elsewhere, the more relevant labor signal came indirectly through PMIs: Korea’s employment component turned positive, while Turkey’s PMI showed faster job cuts and China’s surveys still pointed to weaker hiring conditions. The aggregate read is softer at the margin, but unresolved.

Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
The dominant weekly deterioration came from imported-cost and pipeline-price pressure. Poland’s CPI flash re-accelerated sharply to 3.0% y/y with a 1.0% m/m rise, Brazil’s IGP-M swung back into positive inflation and IPC-Fipe accelerated, China’s NBS and private PMIs both showed much stronger factory-price pressure, and South Africa’s firmer credit/liquidity mix tightened the inflation trade-off. Korea’s CPI undershot forecasts, but only because fuel caps muted immediate oil pass-through, while Turkey’s CPI beat on the headline yet still looked policy-masked and fragile underneath.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
Financial conditions tightened in the broader EM sense because external and policy buffers became less comfortable. Turkey delivered the clearest deterioration, with a sharp fall in gross and especially net reserves materially worsening usable FX protection. South Africa’s stronger private credit growth implied monetary conditions were not restrictive enough to cool demand cleanly, while Brazil’s fiscal and debt backdrop remained constraining despite a better-than-feared monthly budget result. The week’s market-relevant signal was not generalized financial stress, but narrower room to absorb shocks.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
Central-bank room narrowed across the region even where demand was not strong. Poland’s CPI complicated NBP easing, Brazil’s reflationary pipeline and weak fiscal anchor argued against a faster BCB easing path, Korea’s combination of firm manufacturing and fuel-cap-moderated inflation kept BOK patience intact, and India’s oil-sensitive slowdown did not create a clean RBI dovish pivot. China’s better official activity data were offset by private-sector softness and stronger price pressure, while Turkey’s PMI-reserve-CPI mix reinforced that CBRT remained boxed in by external stability and inflation management.

But the embedded caution matters: export-order growth slowed, delivery times lengthened sharply, and price pressures intensified. Why it matters: That is a much harder policy mix than a standard weak-growth/disinflation regime.

Implication: EM central banks now have less room to lean dovish even where demand is softening, because the week’s oil/logistics shock transmission has become too visible to ignore.

<<EM_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 23 Mar 2026 to 27 Mar 2026

A. EM WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

The week resolved into a more differentiated EM regime rather than a single regional easing or inflation story. Growth softened materially in India and Mexico, Poland stayed in a continuation soft-landing bucket, Taiwan remained supported by the tech-manufacturing cycle despite headline moderation, and South Africa still entered the shock phase from improved starting conditions. Labor signals stayed broadly non-threatening where they updated, with Poland and Taiwan offering little evidence of labor-led deterioration. The regional growth message is therefore slower and more uneven, not broad EM contraction.

The more important weekly shift was policy-space dispersion under a shared external cost shock. Mexico printed a stagflationary data mix and still cut, South Africa held and preserved optionality, Brazil’s inflation and external prints stayed comparatively manageable, and Turkey remained the clearest external-liquidity stress case through reserves. That leaves EM in a country-constraint regime: imported energy and input-cost pressure are common, but reaction functions are now being driven much more by domestic credibility, external balance-sheet resilience, and the degree of room each central bank still has.

B. EM MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
The regional growth signal was mixed with a weaker tilt. Mexico’s January IGAE was a real downside shock, India’s flash PMIs deteriorated materially even while remaining above 50, and Poland’s retail data showed continued household expansion but no re-acceleration. Against that, Taiwan’s industrial output remained very strong once calendar effects are handled properly, and South Africa’s leading indicator confirmed firmer starting conditions. EM growth weakened, but not in a synchronized way.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
Labor data did not validate a broad EM deterioration call. Poland’s unemployment rate printed exactly in line at 6.1% and carried no market-relevant shock, while Taiwan’s jobless rate edged down and reinforced a still-firm labor backdrop alongside the tech cycle. No release in the supplied file pointed to a meaningful regional labor break, but the week also did not deliver enough breadth to upgrade labor as a clear strength.

Inflation: ~
Supporting evidence:
Inflation became more uneven rather than uniformly worse. Mexico’s first-half March headline inflation overshot sharply and tightened Banxico’s trade-off, and India’s flash PMI input-cost surge signaled renewed pressure. But South Africa’s PPI stayed soft, Brazil’s IPCA-15 cooled sequentially and on the 12-month rate despite beating consensus, and Poland’s consumption backdrop did not point to overheating. The week’s inflation signal was therefore differentiated, with pressure rising in some economies but not broadening cleanly across EM.

Financial Conditions: ~
Supporting evidence:
Conditions diverged sharply across the region. Turkey’s reserve dynamics kept the external-liquidity story acutely defensive, with net reserves deterioration outweighing the gross bounce. Mexico eased policy by cutting 25bp, Brazil’s external accounts remained comfortable with FDI still covering the current-account gap, and Taiwan’s labor and industrial data did not signal domestic financing strain. The correct regional read is not simple tightening or easing, but selective financial-stress concentration.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
Country-specific constraints tightened even as policy outcomes diverged. Banxico cut into a weaker-growth / firmer-headline-inflation mix, implying more tolerance for near-term inflation risk but also raising validation demands on MXN stability and future prints. SARB held and kept optionality, showing that contained domestic inflation was still not enough to reopen an easy easing path, while Turkey remained balance-sheet constrained through reserves rather than standard cyclical policy logic. Shared external cost pressure did not produce a common response; it increased the importance of domestic constraint sets.

For TWD and the broader EM Asia tech complex, this remains supportive. It preserves the distinction between economies still benefiting from AI capex and external tech demand, and economies already feeling more direct growth or balance-of-payments stress.

Public commentary around the release was essentially absent, which itself is informative. MEXICO — IGAE Economic Activity (Jan)
Release Date: 24 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 13:00

Reuters Data

IGAE Econ Activity YY: -0.3% | Prior: 3.3% | Poll: 1.6% | Surprise: -1.90%

Professional Market Commentary

Institutional framing should be firm here: this was not a small miss, but a real growth downside shock. The official release showed seasonally adjusted activity down 0.9% m/m and only 0.5% y/y, while the original annual series printed -0.3%. The main tape read is that December’s stronger annual print overstated underlying momentum and January stripped that distortion away.

Comment — Economist Layer

This materially worsens the Mexico growth picture. The right hierarchy is: the seasonally adjusted monthly fall is large; the original annual series turned negative; weakness was not confined to one narrow industrial category; and manufacturing remained a major drag while services were only marginally positive. That means the slowdown is broader than the earlier industrial-output miss alone suggested. This is the kind of print that upgrades concern from “sector weakness” to “economy-wide momentum loss.” The growth story was clearly weaker entering the Banxico meeting.

State transition: deterioration

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior Mexico sequence had already become more cautious after the industrial-output miss and the earlier interruption to the disinflation story. January IGAE confirms that the industrial weakness was not just noise. Instead of offsetting the prior downside, the broader activity proxy validates and deepens it.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For Banxico, this was the strongest growth-side argument for a dovish lean that Mexico had printed recently. But it did not stand alone because inflation also updated the same day. The strategic implication was not “easy cut,” but “growth is weakening quickly enough that inflation tolerance and communication discipline matter even more.”

For SARB, this print gave cover for caution rather than panic. It did not force an immediate hawkish reinterpretation of the energy shock, and it supported the case for a central bank that could remain patient while watching whether imported pressure becomes broader domestic inflation.

MEXICO — Interest Rate (Mar)

Professional Market Commentary

This was the week’s clearest policy surprise. Banxico cut 25bp to 6.75% despite the stagflationary data mix printed two days earlier. The official statement makes clear that the Board continued the easing cycle by majority vote, explicitly taking into account the exchange rate, weak activity, and the existing level of monetary restriction.

Comment — Economist Layer

The decision is important precisely because it was not the mechanical response implied by the near-term data. Banxico cut into a backdrop of weaker activity and firmer headline inflation, choosing to lean on the still-contained core path and on the judgment that policy was already sufficiently restrictive. The 3-2 vote split matters because it shows this was not an easy consensus call inside the Board.

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior Mexico blocks had built a genuine stagflationary bind: weaker activity, firmer headline inflation, and less obvious room to ease. The decision reverses the most mechanical reading of that setup. Rather than pausing, Banxico chose to resume easing, which means the reaction function is now more growth-tolerant than the near-term inflation prints alone would have implied.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For MXN and local rates, this is the week’s most consequential EM policy signal. The cut tells markets that Banxico is willing to tolerate a more difficult near-term headline inflation profile if core behaves and activity weakens enough. That makes peso stability and the next inflation prints critical validation tests for whether this renewed easing path can hold.

Implication: Country-specific constraints now matter more than broad EM beta framing.

<<EM_WEEK_BEGIN>>
DATA WINDOW: 16 Mar 2026 to 20 Mar 2026

A. EM WEEK — MACRO SYNTHESIS

EM finished the week in a sharper dispersion regime with the forward constraint worsening. China and Taiwan still provided the strongest growth/external-tech support: China’s Jan-Feb activity block was the clearest upside surprise in the file and Taiwan’s CBC forecasts confirmed an AI-led growth boom. But that strength did not translate into easier policy across the region. Poland remained one of the cleanest domestic disinflation stories, Brazil began easing only in a defensive hawkish-cut format, South Africa’s CPI trough was immediately treated as stale against the oil shock, and Turkey’s reserve depletion marked the clearest deterioration in external-buffer quality. The week-level regime message is therefore mixed-to-resilient domestic activity, broadly non-collapsing labor, but tighter inflation/external constraints and materially narrower easing optionality because the Middle East energy shock has become the dominant forward policy variable.

B. EM MACRO SIGNAL SCOREBOARD

Growth: ~
Supporting evidence:
China’s 16 March activity bundle was the strongest upside growth block of the week, with industrial output at 6.3%, retail sales at 2.8%, and FAI back to +1.8%. Taiwan’s CBC then raised its 2026 GDP forecast sharply to 7.28%, confirming the external-tech boom. Brazil’s January IBC-Br rebounded 0.8%, South Africa retail sales beat strongly at 4.2% y/y, and Poland’s industrial output stabilized. But the growth picture was not cleanly broad-based: China’s property sector remained weak, Poland’s two-month industrial trend still pointed to near-stagnation, and Turkey’s macro story was dominated by external defense rather than cyclical strength.

Labor: ~
Supporting evidence:
The labor signal stayed usable but uneven. India’s unemployment rate improved to 4.9% and urban unemployment edged lower, which removed any immediate growth-collapse read. Poland’s employment remained soft at -0.8% y/y but in a shallow, non-destabilizing way consistent with benign inflation. Against that, China’s urban unemployment rate rose to 5.3%, which prevented a clean recovery interpretation of the activity beat. Across the file, labor did not force easier policy, but it also did not generate a generalized EM demand-break narrative.

Inflation: -
Supporting evidence:
Backward-looking domestic inflation prints were often benign, but the weekly inflation regime worsened on a forward basis. Poland core inflation at 2.5% and PPI at -2.3% y/y kept domestic price pressure subdued, while South Africa CPI fell to 3.0% y/y. But India WPI accelerated to 2.13%, Taiwan lifted its CPI and core CPI forecasts, Brazil’s Copom explicitly reacted more defensively under the geopolitical/oil backdrop, and China’s unchanged LPR decision reflected less urgency to ease as activity improved and imported inflation risk rose. The dominant week signal was not immediate inflation reacceleration everywhere, but a broad tightening of forward inflation constraints.

Financial Conditions: -
Supporting evidence:
Turkey drove the clearest deterioration: net FX reserves fell to $69.0B from $78.78B, confirming active reserve use as an external-shock absorber and marking the sharpest financial-stability warning in the file. Brazil’s 25bp Selic cut versus a 50bp poll expectation also tightened effective financial conditions relative to prior market assumptions by signaling a more defensive reaction function. South Africa’s improving CPI backdrop did not ease conditions because the market shifted quickly to prolonged hold expectations. The region therefore ended the week with more fragile buffers and less supportive market-policy transmission than the backward-looking data alone would suggest.

Policy Constraint: -
Supporting evidence:
The week materially narrowed easing optionality across EM. China’s stronger activity and higher imported-inflation risk froze the near-term case for additional LPR cuts. Taiwan remained on hold but reintroduced latent hawkish asymmetry. Brazil started easing, but only through a hawkish cut that worsened the expected path. South Africa’s CPI success no longer reopened cuts because the oil/rand shock shifted SARB expectations back toward hold. Poland still retained future easing potential, but even there the live constraint had shifted outward toward energy rather than domestic inflation. The regional policy message was therefore tighter constraint, not easier macro management.

MONDAY 16 MAR 2026

CHINA — Urban Investment / Industrial Output / Retail Sales / Unemp Rate Urban Area (Jan-Feb / Feb)
Release Date: 16 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 03:00

Reuters Data

Urban Investment (YTD) YY, Feb: 1.8% | Prior: -3.8% | Poll: -2.1% | Surprise: 3.90%
Industrial Output YY, Feb: 6.3% | Prior: 5.2% | Poll: 5.0% | Surprise: 1.30%
Retail Sales YY, Feb: 2.8% | Prior: 0.9% | Poll: 2.5% | Surprise: 0.30%
Unemp Rate Urban Area, Feb: 5.3% | Prior: 5.1%

Professional Market Commentary

Institutional and official framing treated this as a clear upside activity bundle, but not a clean “China demand problem solved” release. The NBS highlighted a robust start to the year; industrial production accelerated to 6.3%, retail sales improved to 2.8%, and fixed-asset investment swung to +1.8% after a 2025 decline. But the official composition still showed infrastructure doing the heavy lifting, real-estate investment down 11.1%, and urban unemployment ticking up to 5.3%. Reuters framed the release similarly: headline beats were real, but the property sector remained weak.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the strongest upside growth block of the new EM update set, and the FAI surprise is the most important component. The headline swing from a full-year 2025 contraction to +1.8% in Jan-Feb is a genuine macro improvement. But the quality of the improvement remains mixed. Infrastructure rose 11.4% and manufacturing 3.1%, while property investment still fell 11.1%. Industrial production is encouraging, especially the 9.3% rise in equipment manufacturing and 13.1% in high-tech manufacturing, but retail remains vulnerable to Lunar New Year timing noise. The labor signal is the key dissent: unemployment at 5.3%, up from 5.1% in February alone, says the income channel is not confirming a clean recovery yet.

State transition: false dawn risk

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior China sequence had already improved from “deep disinflation / weak nominal tone” toward “less disinflationary, better nominal tone, but still not a self-sustaining private-demand recovery.” The 13 March credit block reinforced that financing remained government-led rather than private-demand-led. Today’s activity beat upgrades the near-term growth optics, but it does not yet overturn that deeper regime diagnosis.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This is supportive for China cyclicals and for the EM growth impulse at the margin, but it is not yet a clean PBOC-easing or private-demand-reflation story. Markets can justify a short-term growth-upgrade narrative, yet the burden of proof shifts to March data: property, labor, prices, and credit will need to confirm that this is more than fiscal-infrastructure stabilization landing in a holiday-distorted window. The external energy shock also makes the next round of China inflation and policy interpretation more complicated than this backward-looking activity beat alone suggests.

INDIA — WPI Inflation YY (Feb)
Release Date: 16 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 07:30

Reuters Data

WPI Inflation YY, Feb: 2.13% | Prior: 1.81% | Poll: 2.00% | Surprise: 0.130%

Professional Market Commentary

The official DPIIT release makes the composition clear: WPI rose to 2.13% because manufacturing, especially basic metals, together with non-food articles, food articles, and textiles, all firmed further. Primary articles accelerated to 3.27%, fuel and power stayed negative at -3.78% but became less deflationary, and manufactured products rose to 2.92%. This keeps the institutional focus on WPI as the faster oil-sensitive inflation channel, especially relative to CPI.

Comment — Economist Layer

The February print is not alarming in isolation, but the trajectory is clearly deteriorating. The key analytical wedge is CPI versus WPI. CPI was still only 3.21% in the prior India block, so the RBI’s target variable remains contained. But WPI is now accelerating through upstream goods and metals, which is where imported energy and supply-chain pass-through usually appears first. This means India’s inflation constraint is becoming more pipeline-driven, not yet consumer-endogenous. That matters because it narrows the space for an easy dovish read without yet forcing an RBI hawkish turn.

State transition: deterioration

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior India sequence had already moved toward “contained CPI, but easing space narrowing.” February WPI extends that exact structure. It does not create a new inflation regime, but it further weakens any clean residual-easing narrative if energy costs stay elevated.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For rates and FX, the message is conditional: India remains one of the stronger EM growth stories, but the inflation side is becoming less forgiving. As long as CPI stays moderate, RBI can still avoid a hawkish shift. But if March and April CPI begin to absorb what WPI is already signaling, the easing path narrows materially.

Reuters Data

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For RBI reaction-function mapping, this labor release is secondary but directionally important: it does not create urgency to ease. That leaves inflation — especially oil pass-through from upstream prices into CPI — as the dominant near-term policy variable.

POLAND — Current Account (Jan)
Release Date: 16 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 14:00

Reuters Data

Current Account, Jan: 1,153M | Prior: -1,698M | Poll: -330M | Surprise: 1,483.0M

Professional Market Commentary

The headline current-account surprise was very large, but institutional commentary immediately qualified it. ING highlighted that exports fell 1.8% y/y and imports dropped 6.1% y/y, with the swing helped by one fewer working day and severe winter conditions that disrupted transport and logistics. On a 12-month basis the current-account deficit improved to 0.6% of GDP from 0.7%.

This is an external-balance improvement, but not a strong-growth signal. That matters because a headline CA beat can otherwise be over-read as macro strength. Here it is better interpreted as an additional external buffer in an economy already moving through a benign inflation regime.

State transition: improvement

Context from prior regional live file:
The prior Poland story had already become one of orderly disinflation and increasing room for policy easing. This current-account surprise adds resilience, but it does not change the core macro narrative as much as the inflation data do.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

PLN implications are modest relative to the inflation/policy story. The more useful strategic read is that Poland entered the energy-shock phase with a somewhat better external cushion than feared, which helps distinguish it from the more vulnerable EM importers.

POLAND — Net Inflation YY (Feb)
Release Date: 16 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 14:00

Reuters Data

Net Inflation YY, Feb: 2.50% | Prior: 2.70% | Poll: 2.60% | Surprise: -0.100%

Professional Market Commentary

This is one of the cleaner domestic disinflation prints in the regional tape. Core inflation fell to 2.5% y/y from 2.7%, the lowest since late 2019 according to the NBP-sourced series. That reinforces the existing institutional view that Poland remains on a comparatively benign inflation path, even if higher energy prices complicate the forward easing debate.

Comment — Economist Layer

This is the more important Poland release today. The current-account surprise is noisy; core inflation at 2.5% is the cleaner policy signal. Underlying domestic inflation pressure is now materially softer, which is exactly what a central bank wants to see if it is to maintain easing optionality later in the year. The caveat is obvious: this is backward-looking domestic disinflation landing just as the external energy shock threatens to rebuild imported-price pressure. But as of February itself, Poland still looks like one of the least inflation-constrained stories in the EM set.

State transition: improvement

Context from prior regional live file:
The 13 March Poland CPI final had already confirmed an orderly disinflation regime and shifted the main debate outward toward energy rather than inward toward domestic price pressure. The 2.5% net inflation print deepens that same story rather than changing it.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

If the energy shock fades, Poland remains one of the cleaner easing candidates in the region. If it persists, the likely adjustment is to pace and communication rather than to the basic direction of the disinflation story. That makes Poland an important contrast case inside EM dispersion.

WEDNESDAY 18 MAR 2026

SOUTH AFRICA — CPI (Feb)
Release Date: 18 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 09:00

Reuters Data

CPI MM, Feb: 0.4% | Prior: 0.2% | Poll: 0.5% | Surprise: -0.10%
CPI YY, Feb: 3.0% | Prior: 3.5% | Poll: 3.1% | Surprise: -0.10%

Professional Market Commentary

Institutional commentary treated this as an excellent backward-looking inflation print that arrived too late to ease the policy debate. Stats SA’s CPI release showed inflation at 3.0% y/y and 0.4% m/m, with the disinflation driven by delayed medical-aid increases, weaker fuel prices, and softer food inflation. But professional commentary immediately shifted forward: higher oil prices and rand weakness are expected to push inflation back up from April, and the 26 March SARB meeting is now widely seen as a hold rather than a cut.

Comment — Economist Layer

The mechanical achievement is real: headline CPI reached the SARB’s 3% point target. But analytically this is one of the clearest “good data, bad forward regime” prints of the week. The February trough was helped by one-off or timing-sensitive elements — especially delayed medical-aid implementation and sharply negative fuel inflation — while the oil shock is already set to reverse part of that relief in April. So this is a genuine inflation success and simultaneously a stale policy signal.

State transition: false dawn

Context from prior regional live file:
The earlier South Africa sequence had improved meaningfully on the back of the Q4 current-account swing into surplus plus better mining and manufacturing data. This CPI print confirms that South Africa entered March with a stronger disinflation backdrop than previously assumed, but it also shows that the easing-friendly interpretation is now being interrupted by the energy shock before the SARB can really exploit it.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

For SARB, the bar for hikes is still high, but the door to cuts has effectively shut for now. The relevant market signal is not that inflation touched target; it is that the target hit is already being treated as backward-looking. South Africa therefore shifts from a potential near-term easing story toward a prolonged-hold story, with the April fuel-price adjustment the next key verification point.

This is a strong print on its own terms. A 1.7pp beat versus consensus is large, and the composition suggests real discretionary spending strength rather than only a narrow essentials bounce. State transition: improvement

The earlier South Africa activity sequence had already turned more constructive through mining, manufacturing, and the external accounts. Retail now extends that better domestic-demand story. But for markets, CPI and the SARB path matter more than one backward-looking retail beat. The strategic value of the release is therefore mostly to soften the growth downside, not to reopen a cut narrative.

BRAZIL — Selic Interest Rate (18 Mar)
Release Date: 18 Mar 2026 | Local Time: 22:30

Reuters Data

Selic Interest Rate: 14.75% | Prior: 15.00% | Poll: 14.50% | Surprise: +0.25pp

Professional Market Commentary

Institutional and official commentary treated this as a hawkish cut rather than a dovish opening of the easing cycle. Copom cut the Selic by 25bp to 14.75%, beginning easing but delivering less than the 50bp cut implied by the Reuters poll. The committee cited intensified geopolitical conflict in the Middle East, emphasized “serenity and cautiousness,” and deliberately avoided explicit forward guidance. Reuters also highlighted that the central bank lifted its 2026 inflation projection to 3.9% from 3.4% and its Q3 2027 policy-horizon projection to 3.3% from 3.2%.

Comment — Economist Layer

The key to this decision is asymmetry. Brazil had enough disinflation and enough activity slowing to begin cutting, but not enough confidence to validate a clean easing path. A 25bp move preserved the January signal that March easing would start, while minimizing the risk of a fast credibility loss under an oil shock. So the headline says “cycle started,” but the deeper signal is that the Copom is no longer confident in the pre-war easing template. This is not a normal first cut. It is a constrained, optionality-preserving cut delivered into a worsening external inflation backdrop.

State transition: deterioration

Context from prior regional live file:
The earlier Brazil sequence had moved from pipeline disinflation and weak Q4 growth toward mixed-but-resilient domestic activity, leaving Copom room to start easing. The January IBC-Br block reinforced that “weak enough to cut, not weak enough to panic” setup. The 18 March decision keeps that baseline but materially worsens the expected easing path: the cycle has begun, yet with a more defensive policy reaction function than the prior week implied.

HF Take — Strategist Layer

This should be read as a hawkish cut for BRL and front-end rates. The next question is no longer whether easing starts, but whether April can deliver 50bp or whether the cycle effectively stalls at 25bp increments. Brazil remains on the easing side of the EM policy split, but much less cleanly than markets were assuming a week ago.

Reuters also reported that Governor Yang said prolonged Middle East conflict could force tighter policy in Q2, which inserted a latent tightening option into what had previously been a pure hold regime.

Comment — Economist Layer

The hold is fully in line; the analytical weight falls on the forecast revision. At the same time, the inflation revision is modest rather than alarming, which means the CBC can remain on hold while quietly reintroducing hawkish optionality if the energy shock proves persistent. State transition: continuation

Context from prior regional live file:
